  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_573c3b74-713a-4bbd-80c8-3a368b885fce'

<div id="review_1256219079" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1256219079" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1256219079?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1256219079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer667895090640739310">In the brief prose piece <i>The Four Cycles</i> <b>Jorge Luis Borges</b> wrote that there are only four stories in the world: the story of war, the story of return, the story of search and the story of sacrifice (Troy, Ulysses, Jason, Prometheus). <br/>“Four are the stories. During the time left to us we will continue telling them, transformed.”<br/>And there is no other writer who can retell these four stories the way <b>Jorge Luis Borges</b> does, transforming them into intellectual labyrinths and scholarly conundrums.<br/><blockquote>Like </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText667895090640739310" style="display:none">In the brief prose piece <i>The Four Cycles</i> <b>Jorge Luis Borges</b> wrote that there are only four stories in the world: the story of war, the story of return, the story of search and the story of sacrifice (Troy, Ulysses, Jason, Prometheus). <br />“Four are the stories. During the time left to us we will continue telling them, transformed.”<br />And there is no other writer who can retell these four stories the way <b>Jorge Luis Borges</b> does, transforming them into intellectual labyrinths and scholarly conundrums.<br /><blockquote>Like all the men of the Library, in my younger days I traveled; I have journeyed in quest of a book, perhaps the catalog of catalogs. Now that my eyes can hardly make out what I myself have written, I am preparing to die, a few leagues from the hexagon where I was born. When I am dead, compassionate hands will throw me over the railing; my tomb will be the unfathomable air, my body will sink for ages, and will decay and dissolve in the wind engendered by my fall, which shall be infinite.</blockquote> <br />He turns the universe into <i>The Library of Babel</i>, the probability theory into <i>The Garden of Forking Paths</i> and the first letter of the Hebrew alphabet into a locus of all the magic – <i>Aleph</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="667895090640739310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_180246755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180246755" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180246755?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=lists-glorious-lists">lists-glorious-lists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=linguistics-and-philosophy">linguistics-and-philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=the-goodreads-experience">the-goodreads-experience</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=mentions-twilight">mentions-twilight</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180246755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1944594872512794065">In Borges's short story, the world consists of a gigantic library which contains every possible book that can ever be written. So, somewhere, there must logically be <i>the</i> book, the one that reveals the Library's secret! Unfortunately, there is no filing system, and no one has any idea of how to find the elusive book. In fact, it's challenging even to locate one which contains a meaningful sentence: most of them are gibberish from beginning to end. <br/><br/>Well, our own world isn't quite as bad - but it's</span>
  <span id="freeText1944594872512794065" style="display:none">In Borges's short story, the world consists of a gigantic library which contains every possible book that can ever be written. So, somewhere, there must logically be <i>the</i> book, the one that reveals the Library's secret! Unfortunately, there is no filing system, and no one has any idea of how to find the elusive book. In fact, it's challenging even to locate one which contains a meaningful sentence: most of them are gibberish from beginning to end. <br /><br />Well, our own world isn't quite as bad - but it's still harder than it should be to locate the books you <i>really</i> want to read, when they're mixed up with the ones you just think you <i>might</i> want to read. I am often appalled at the amount of time I waste on this site, but comfort myself with the thought that it has helped me find some amazing books I normally wouldn't even have considered. <br /><br />But exactly how helpful has it been? The other day, it occurred to me to try and answer this question quantitatively. I calculate that, since I started hanging out here in late 2008, I have read 42 books just because someone here has recommended them. (I didn't count books recommended by people on Goodreads whom I also know in real life, otherwise the figure would be considerably higher). After some more thought, I've picked out a Top Ten, which I present here for your amusement:<br /><br />10. I've never seen anyone outside Goodreads mention <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129083403" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Everything Explained Through Flowcharts</b>
</a>, recommended to me by David G, but it's the funniest thing I've seen in ages. I challenge you to read it without giggling helplessly at least a couple of times. Why it isn't more famous is more than I understand.<br /><br />9. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113257800" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>À rebours</b>
</a>, a weird 19th century French novel recommended to me by Sabrina, is another book that deserves to be better known. Nothing happens, but it's somehow utterly compelling. I think it's also been very influential.<br /><br />8. I love books written under strong formalist constraints, but I'd never heard of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44515917" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Eunoia</b>
</a>, recommended by Gary. Five chapters, each using only one vowel, and, even though it sounds impossible, it works remarkably well as poetry. Really!<br /><br />7. Eric W recommended <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47990308" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>The Terrible Hours: The Greatest Submarine Rescue in History</b>
</a>. If you're after inspiration and good old-fashioned heroism, look no further. <br /><br />6. Choupette was so indignant about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46844401" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Plateforme</b>
</a> that I had to check it out for myself. I liked it enough that I also read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39269005" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Les particules élémentaires</b>
</a>. I won't promise that you'll <i>enjoy</i> them, but they're certainly going to make you think.<br /><br />5. Everyone recommended <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112088196" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>The Shallows: What the Internet Is Doing to Our Brains</b>
</a>. Alas, all too true. The mere fact that I'm sitting here writing this proves his point.<br /><br />4. Would you believe it, I hadn't even heard of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39762831" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Infinite Jest</b>
</a> before I joined GR. Within a couple of months, I'd given in and bought a copy. Admittedly, I also bought a copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38510522" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Twilight</b>
</a> at the same time...<br /><br />3. Pavel told me I had to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106349620" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Voices from Chernobyl</b>
</a>, and he was right. Whatever your opinions on nuclear power, it's irresponsible not to. You can't take more than a chapter or so at a time; after that, you just sit there stunned, doing your best not to cry. Another book that people have unaccountably overlooked.<br /><br />2. Was I really going to read a thousand page physics text full of scary math? I did a math degree in the late 70s, but this looked way over my level. However, Nick called me chicken enough times that I decided to tackle <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60011150" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>The Road to Reality: A Complete Guide to the Laws of the Universe</b>
</a>. I've finally got to the end, and wow, was it a fascinating read! If you like math and physics, take Nick's advice: forget the pop science books and go for the big one. It's worth the effort.<br /><br />1. I don't really know Norwegian, and how likely was it that I'd buy a three volume magical-realist Norwegian novel by an author I'd never heard of? But, moved by Oriana's glowing review, I started thinking that I speak Swedish, Norwegian isn't that different (it's a kind of Spanish/Portugese deal), so why not give it a shot? By the time I was 20 pages into <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48294943" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Forføreren</b>
</a>, I was hooked, and then I immediately continued with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52296128" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Erobreren</b>
</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52295642" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Oppdageren</b>
</a>. The trilogy is the most brilliant thing I have read this century, and I can't recommend it highly enough. Thank you Oriana!<br /><br />So, there you are, and I hope I've made at least one sale :) In the interests of completeness, here's the rest of the list, in alphabetical order:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95508776" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>99 Ways to Tell a Story: Exercises in Style</b>
</a><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172236023" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>The Authoritarians</b>
</a><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112686591" rel="nofollow noopener">

<div id="review_1777345033" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1777345033" />

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=made-me-think">made-me-think</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=the-shorts">the-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=classics">classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1777345033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4897321021580688418">I read "The Library of Babel," one of Jorge Luis Borges’ most famous stories, as part of the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/426504.Ficciones' title='Ficciones by Jorge Luis Borges' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ficciones</a> collection. “The Library of Babel” posits a universe in the form of a library made out of connected hexagonal rooms, each room filled with books and the barest necessities for life. Each book contains 410 pages, with 40 lines of 80 letters each. There are 25 letters and punctuation marks in the alphabet. The Library contains every possible combination of those letters. Most of the books are com</span>
  <span id="freeText4897321021580688418" style="display:none">I read "The Library of Babel," one of Jorge Luis Borges’ most famous stories, as part of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/426504.Ficciones" title="Ficciones by Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Ficciones</a> collection. “The Library of Babel” posits a universe in the form of a library made out of connected hexagonal rooms, each room filled with books and the barest necessities for life. Each book contains 410 pages, with 40 lines of 80 letters each. There are 25 letters and punctuation marks in the alphabet. The Library contains every possible combination of those letters. Most of the books are complete gibberish, of course, but like the Infinite Monkey Theorem says, if you have enough monkeys banging away on typewriters for long enough (i.e., infinite time and infinite monkeys), eventually they’ll write <i>Hamlet</i>.<br /><br />But life for the people dwelling in this library is profoundly frustrating, even depressing, since only a vanishingly small percentage of the books make any sense at all. Borges explores the ways that people might react to this, with several nods to religion and philosophy. There's not any real plot to this story; it feels more like an essay or an intellectual exercise ("How would people react if...").<br /><br />Mathematicians have had a field day with this book’s concept, figuring out how many books such a library would contain. Per <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Library_of_Babel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia’s article on this story</a>, there would be far more books in this library (1.956 x 10 to the 1,834,097th power) than there are thought to be atoms in the observable universe (10 to the 80th power). It's mind-boggling.<br /><br />But this story is not so much about the numbers, as about what it would be like to live in this intriguing but highly frustrating world. <br /><br />You can read a copy of this story <a href="http://www.arts.ucsb.edu/faculty/reese/classes/artistsbooks/The%20Library%20of%20Babel.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</span>

          </span>

    <a title="Sr3yas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23097409-sr3yas"><img alt="Sr3yas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520526764p2/23097409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2112512122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8093905867867676281"><b>Hey, you. Yea, I am talking to you!</b> Do you want to get freaked out by the sheer magnitude of an idea that's right in front of you? Step right in!<br/><br/>In this short story, Author Jorge Luis Borges envisions a universe in the form of a vast library, a library of meticulous pattern and structure. In this library, you can find an incomprehensible number of hexagonal rooms with a specific number of books: Books that contain all knowledge of the universe. But here is the catch: <i>
  <b>All this knowledge is mix</b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText8093905867867676281" style="display:none"><b>Hey, you. Yea, I am talking to you!</b> Do you want to get freaked out by the sheer magnitude of an idea that's right in front of you? Step right in!<br><br>In this short story, Author Jorge Luis Borges envisions a universe in the form of a vast library, a library of meticulous pattern and structure. In this library, you can find an incomprehensible number of hexagonal rooms with a specific number of books: Books that contain all knowledge of the universe. But here is the catch: <i>
  <b>All this knowledge is mixed with utter gibberish.</b>
</i> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504459129i/23758839._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The author, Mr. Borges, was a librarian himself and it is safe to assume that the inspiration of his unique universe came from his surroundings, and it is quite brilliant. He reflects the flaws of human kind subtly here in form of rogue librarians, mystical legends, and slow madness. <br><br>Nevertheless, the short story itself is filled with bits of Babel as it's more of a detailed description of an alternative universe and not a story. I was going to give a three stars rating and move on with my life.<br><br>But then I found a Website.<br><br>I think I am late to the party, but two years before, a man named <i>Jonathan Basile</i> created the <b>library of Babel online</b> using an algorithm. Any English sentence/paragraph of length 3200 characters can be found this library mixed with Babel. There are about <i>10^5000</i> books in this library <i>(Age of Earth is 10^17 seconds).</i> To understand What's actually happening, you might need to watch a Youtube video. But let me tell you something, <b>
  <i>the library contains every sentence you have ever said or will say in future, It contains every book ever written or will be written, It contains the first words you ever said and last words you will say, it contains secrets to the universe and unfortunately or fortunately, it contains a lot of gibberish.</i>
</b><br><br>And before even I wrote this review, these sentences were there in Library of Babel.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504458906i/23758779._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>*Zoom this image*</i><br><br>It's no trick, it's just about exhausting every possible combination the 26 letters of the alphabet can create!</span>

<div id="review_2089397606" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2089397606" />
    <a title="Cyndi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7132123-cyndi"><img alt="Cyndi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510671513p2/7132123.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2089397606?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cyndi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cyndi" href="/user/show/7132123-cyndi">Cyndi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2089397606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17107304847394477010">So, this is a short story, but there is so much in it that I reread it a half dozen times, found a few audio readings and looked up summaries trying to grasp the whole story. Basically its weird, but cool. <br/> In this guys universe the world is made up of libraries. Each room is a hexagon with two small closets. One is a bathroom and the other is a room to sleep standing up. People are born, live and die in these rooms. <br/> Now here is where it gets really bad. There are only four shelves of books in </span>
  <span id="freeText17107304847394477010" style="display:none">So, this is a short story, but there is so much in it that I reread it a half dozen times, found a few audio readings and looked up summaries trying to grasp the whole story. Basically its weird, but cool. <br /> In this guys universe the world is made up of libraries. Each room is a hexagon with two small closets. One is a bathroom and the other is a room to sleep standing up. People are born, live and die in these rooms. <br /> Now here is where it gets really bad. There are only four shelves of books in each room and most are in languages hard to decipher. When they are finally deciphered the are gibberish. <br /> No fiction, none of your favorite authors. No wonder they are suicidal. <br /> There are some wanderers who travel from hexagon to hexagon looking for the perfect book. (Which they never find) and there are others on a pilgrimage looking for a magical book that was read by a messiah. There is also a group of dissenters who destroy books that they disagree with. But the author says that doesn't matter because there are hundreds of copies of each book spread throughout. <br /> This is a short story and definitely worth a read.😊</span>
  <a data-text-id="17107304847394477010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284403193" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7454072577478579799">“<b>For a book to exist, it is sufficient that it is <i>possible</i>. Only the impossible is excluded</b>.”<br/><br/>Paradoxes abound in this allegory that has aspects of The Blind Watchmaker, especially DNA, and also the <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_monkey_theorem' rel='nofollow noopener'>Infinite Monkey Theorem</a>. <br/><br/>I have the Collected Fictions (with copious translator's notes), but am splitting my review of that into its components, in publication order: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1252822660' rel='nofollow noopener'>Collected Fictions - all reviews</a>. This is one of the the longer stories in The Garden of Forking Paths, published in 1941.<br/><br/>The universe</span>
  <span id="freeText7454072577478579799" style="display:none">“<b>For a book to exist, it is sufficient that it is <i>possible</i>. Only the impossible is excluded</b>.”<br /><br />Paradoxes abound in this allegory that has aspects of The Blind Watchmaker, especially DNA, and also the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_monkey_theorem" rel="nofollow noopener">Infinite Monkey Theorem</a>. <br /><br />I have the Collected Fictions (with copious translator's notes), but am splitting my review of that into its components, in publication order: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1252822660" rel="nofollow noopener">Collected Fictions - all reviews</a>. This is one of the the longer stories in The Garden of Forking Paths, published in 1941.<br /><br />The universe is an infinite Library. Maybe the universe is the internet? But Borges’ library is more beautiful: an endless series of connecting, identical, hexagons, and it has - and will - exist for eternity. <br /><br />Each vestibule has “a mirror which faithfully duplicates appearances”, leading men to infer that the Library is not infinite, otherwise “what need would there be for that illusory replication?”<br /><br />But it is infinite: the books contain “all that is able to be expressed, in every language”, composed of the same alphabetic elements, and each is unique. But are uniqueness and infinity contradictory?<br /><br />Most of the books are indecipherable, and “trying to find sense in books” is “a vain and superstitious habit”, likened to palmistry and numerology. Surely that doesn’t apply to this, or does it? (Recursion, again.)<br /><br />“You who read me – are you certain you understand my language?”<br /><br />“Man, the imperfect librarian, may be the work of chance or malevolent demiurges; the universe… can only be the work of a god.” That’s “a god”, not “God”. <br /><br />Who or what made me? Am I real, or just making marks on one of an infinite number of pages that may never be read?<br /><br />These ideas of infinity are explored and elaborated on in “Undr” and The Mirror and the Mask, which throw minimalism into the mix. More specifically, the story of The Book of Sand is like the Library of Babel in miniature: a single, infinite book. They are all in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1322888793" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Sand</a>.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8541761422727593791">Found this to be a great analogy to the world we live in. Everyone seems to have the answer to all of life's problems, but the issue is it's not so simple to sort through all of the variables when you have little to no means of measuring each option.   <span id="freeText8541761422727593791" style="display:none">Found this to be a great analogy to the world we live in. Everyone seems to have the answer to all of life's problems, but the issue is it's not so simple to sort through all of the variables when you have little to no means of measuring each option. That's pretty much how I read this short story, in life it is feasible to live the 'perfect' life, since the variables are there, however since there is no distinctive guide to do so, we are forced to do our best to sort through the gibberish (in the story, being the books which made sense no matter how you looked at them) to opportunities that may have a glimpse of hope for positive results.<br /><br />So yeah, we live in a world like The Library of Babel, where potentially the answer to all of life's issues are out there somewhere, but since we have no means of locating it, we're stuck in the chaos that is existence as we know it.<br /><br />The build-up of imagery at the beginning was also spectacular. Cheers Borges!</span>

<div id="review_2058573576" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2058573576" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2058573576?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2058573576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer555512028727084024">Borges's philosophical short-story describes the universe in terms of an infinite library constructed in a series of hexagon galleries in which the books contain every possible combination of letters, spaces and punctuation marks providing a metaphor for thinking about knowledge and truth. As a paradox of infinite possibilities, some of the volumes within turn out to be what appears to be complete gibberish, Some go nuts from the despair of trying to logically understand and catalogue every book</span>
  <span id="freeText555512028727084024" style="display:none">Borges's philosophical short-story describes the universe in terms of an infinite library constructed in a series of hexagon galleries in which the books contain every possible combination of letters, spaces and punctuation marks providing a metaphor for thinking about knowledge and truth. As a paradox of infinite possibilities, some of the volumes within turn out to be what appears to be complete gibberish, Some go nuts from the despair of trying to logically understand and catalogue every book in the library, whilst some take a leap of faith. Beyond the abstract intentions, Borges was also expressing the angst of simply being lost in the universe, and of not being able to understand it. In other words, the limited knowledge of this infinite library by the narrator inhabiting this vast space reflects Borges’s own uncertainty about Life and the Universe, the nature of hope, and the creation of meaning. It's quite simply quintessential Borges, and not a bad place to start for the newbie, although, it still feels like throwing yourself in at the deep end. He evokes a sense of wonder and the infinite possibilities that go with it like nobody else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="555512028727084024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

A Kaleidoscope of earlier ideas like Borel's dactylographic monkey theorem, Pascal's metaphor and Robert Burton's variations, a mathematical thought experiment with infinities and labyrinths that employs cabalistic reasoning which blurs the infinite and the finite with philosophical implications that puts the Gita in mind, a melting pot of motifs that would influence Eco's influential mast</span>
  <span id="freeText2712465665094299361" style="display:none"><br /><i>You who read me - are you certain you understand my language?</i><br /><br />Understanding? Certain? Wouldn't even pretend. A Kaleidoscope of earlier ideas like Borel's dactylographic monkey theorem, Pascal's metaphor and Robert Burton's variations, a mathematical thought experiment with infinities and labyrinths that employs cabalistic reasoning which blurs the infinite and the finite with philosophical implications that puts the Gita in mind, a melting pot of motifs that would influence Eco's influential masterpiece - the name of the rose, and a strange allegory that had me reading all over the internet and pondering for hours I know not how many. And all these in a short-story. Moving his bigger works to top-priority.</span>

<div id="review_1381355663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1381355663" />
    <a title="Leonard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4940476-leonard"><img alt="Leonard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406152730p2/4940476.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1381355663?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonard" href="/user/show/4940476-leonard">Leonard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4940476-leonard?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4940476-leonard?shelf=literature-spanish">literature-spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1381355663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3722663695087610657">Jorge Luis Borges, one of the most imaginative writers I have come across, could have been a mathematician, a physicist, a philosopher or a theologian. I can see his influence on Umberto Eco in the manipulation of text and the blending between fiction and reality. To read Borges is to immerse myself in a magical world where the concept of infinity manifests in space and time, where the boundary between dream and reality fades, where the past and the future converge into an instant, where levels </span>
  <span id="freeText3722663695087610657" style="display:none">Jorge Luis Borges, one of the most imaginative writers I have come across, could have been a mathematician, a physicist, a philosopher or a theologian. I can see his influence on Umberto Eco in the manipulation of text and the blending between fiction and reality. To read Borges is to immerse myself in a magical world where the concept of infinity manifests in space and time, where the boundary between dream and reality fades, where the past and the future converge into an instant, where levels of texts superimpose on one another, where fiction imitates nonfiction and life is a drama on stage. To read Borges is to become children again, listening to stories of magic and wonder, of unfathomable worlds. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1213638002l/172366.jpg" width="160" height="240" alt="The Library of Babel" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In “The Library of Babel,” Borges again plays around with the concept of infinity, but this time also with combinatorial and I can imagine Borges as a mathematician or computer scientist. A labyrinth of infinite number of rooms stores books that include all combinations of a 22-letter alphabet plus spaces and the comma and period. Since we know the number of characters in each book, we can calculate the number of possible books (not infinite). Of course, most of them are meaningless. Is this universe of repeated rooms each with five shelves and thirty-five books a mirror of our world? Interestingly, in Eco’s The Name of the Rose, the blind monk who oversees the library is named Jorge of Burges.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1300663961p8/236551.jpg" width="160" height="240" alt="Jorge Luis Borges" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I recommend Borges to anyone who wants to dream of magical worlds, who wants to reflect on reality and fiction, who wants to analyze the boundary between text and the interpreter, and who wants to contemplate on the nature of infinity. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3722663695087610657" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6476832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer528398383459760448">Easily one of the strangest books I've ever read. I actually ordered it by accident, thinking it was an anthology. But actually this entire slender volume is devoted to one Borges short story, complete with beautiful etchings showing that his impossible library is actually possible. While it's not worth the cover price for everyone, anyone who dismissed his fictional library should flip through these pages and see that he wasn't writing flippantly. As "Library of Babel" was possibly Borges' most</span>
  <span id="freeText528398383459760448" style="display:none">Easily one of the strangest books I've ever read. I actually ordered it by accident, thinking it was an anthology. But actually this entire slender volume is devoted to one Borges short story, complete with beautiful etchings showing that his impossible library is actually possible. While it's not worth the cover price for everyone, anyone who dismissed his fictional library should flip through these pages and see that he wasn't writing flippantly. As "Library of Babel" was possibly Borges' most imaginative piece of fiction, it's wonderful to see it given shape - as wonderful as it is unexpected.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9115277972287184640">This story is not fiction. The library of babel exists. You can view it here: <a>https://libraryofbabel.info/</a><br/><br/><br/>At present, the site has catalogued every possible combination of 3200 characters. You can read how its done on the site – its really incredible. You can type in any paragraph, from the opening of your own novel to the end of your favorite thriller, and find that it already exists in a specific page of a specific book on a specific shelf in the library.<br/><br/>Navigating the site is an eerie experie</span>
  <span id="freeText9115277972287184640" style="display:none">This story is not fiction. The library of babel exists. You can view it here: <a>https://libraryofbabel.info/</a><br /><br /><br />At present, the site has catalogued every possible combination of 3200 characters. You can read how its done on the site – its really incredible. You can type in any paragraph, from the opening of your own novel to the end of your favorite thriller, and find that it already exists in a specific page of a specific book on a specific shelf in the library.<br /><br />Navigating the site is an eerie experience. You can look through pages upon pages of gibberish, encountering random words, mysterious strings of letters. I highly recommend you spend a little while getting acquainted with the library. Every conversation you have had or will ever have exists there. <br /><br />A precise description of the manner and date of your death exists in the library. <br /><br />A precise description of what your friends really think about you exists in the library.<br /><br />They already exist, waiting to be uncovered, amongst unimaginable piles of gibberish and fragments.<br /><br />I only wonder what Borges would've thought of it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9115277972287184640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1071844673" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1071844673" />
    <a title="Martyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4795604-martyn"><img alt="Martyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487803251p2/4795604.jpg" /></a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4795604-martyn?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4795604-martyn?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4795604-martyn?shelf=best-in-show">best-in-show</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1071844673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5482812615092050011">This is a fantastic and thought provoking book. I first heard of it while reading <a href='http://firstmonday.org/ojs/index.php/fm/article/view/3237/3416' rel='nofollow noopener'>this essay</a> by Christopher Rowe.<br/><br/>I know that Borges is really discussing the history, and completeness, of human knowledge but his essay, as Rowe suggests, has marked implications for those trying to create a universal library today. Such entities might include Google or Amazon, amongst others. The sheer futility of gathering every last letter of every last book that has ever existed, or that could possibly have exis</span>
  <span id="freeText5482812615092050011" style="display:none">This is a fantastic and thought provoking book. I first heard of it while reading <a href="http://firstmonday.org/ojs/index.php/fm/article/view/3237/3416" rel="nofollow noopener">this essay</a> by Christopher Rowe.<br /><br />I know that Borges is really discussing the history, and completeness, of human knowledge but his essay, as Rowe suggests, has marked implications for those trying to create a universal library today. Such entities might include Google or Amazon, amongst others. The sheer futility of gathering every last letter of every last book that has ever existed, or that could possibly have existed, is put across quite clearly by Borges.<br /><br />The resulting chaos of having to wade through mountains of garbage to find the occasional book that is readable, or desirable, seems to point directly to our modern experience with the internet. The internet is viewed as having the potential to contain "everything". Whether that is even something we should be striving for is not even open for discussion at present as the digitizers run rampant with information.<br /><br />I guess I should be happy that librarians will become more, not less, necessary in the digital world in order to help confused souls navigate their way to useful information amongst the sea of dross and because they will be needed to navigate and critically analyze that dross themselves in order to create robust bibliographies for digi-patrons.<br /><br />The start of my library science studies have proved a remarkable place for philosophical thinking, thankfully. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5482812615092050011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Watching events unfold before me like a 3D TV through time and space.<br/><br/>I saw the history of a collective civilization, their peak and demise, their wars and pilgrimages. Seeking to find meaning in a chaotic world.<br/><br/>It was so weird, yet so peaceful and nostalgic at the same time... Nostalgic for something I've never known before.<br/><br/>That's Borges for you.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2467436045" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2467436045" />
    <a title="Jake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6968193-jake"><img alt="Jake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522915146p2/6968193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2467436045?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jake" href="/user/show/6968193-jake">Jake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2467436045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8112695125050922867">All books that will ever be written exist, we have just not created them yet. This is what Borges postulates with this short story. My initial reaction to reading this is that it is a mildly arrogant viewpoint. But then again, is it really less arrogant than us saying that we were the ones that wrote the book, that it was generated by the human mind? I don’t think we can answer for certain either of these things. What I do know is that this is a damn good short story.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3359955446" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3359955446" />
    <a title="Kateblue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76979971-kateblue"><img alt="Kateblue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600299387p2/76979971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3359955446?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kateblue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kateblue" href="/user/show/76979971-kateblue">Kateblue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76979971-kateblue?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3359955446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9778368836107458304">Interesting allegory written in 1941 about a library that seems to be an entire universe. Glad it wasn't longer because it was pretty crunchy. Apparently there has been much intellectual discussion about it over the years. <br/><br/>Try it! I found it online here for free here: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://maskofreason.files.wordpress.com/2011/02/the-library-of-babel-by-jorge-luis-borges.pdf'>https://maskofreason.files.wordpress....</a><br/><br/>Added 5 28--Here's an interesting Wikipedia argument about the story and some of the concepts within--<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Library_of_Babel'>https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Lib...</a></span>
  <span id="freeText9778368836107458304" style="display:none">Interesting allegory written in 1941 about a library that seems to be an entire universe. Glad it wasn't longer because it was pretty crunchy. Apparently there has been much intellectual discussion about it over the years. <br /><br />Try it! I found it online here for free here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://maskofreason.files.wordpress.com/2011/02/the-library-of-babel-by-jorge-luis-borges.pdf">https://maskofreason.files.wordpress....</a><br /><br />Added 5 28--Here's an interesting Wikipedia argument about the story and some of the concepts within--<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Library_of_Babel">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Lib...</a></span>

<div id="review_3006672536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3006672536" />
    <a title="Carly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81413177-carly"><img alt="Carly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572926287p2/81413177.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3006672536?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carly" href="/user/show/81413177-carly">Carly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3006672536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16786817710461195518"> The concept of infinity never fails to perplex me. Previously, my visions of infinity were focused mainly on the idea of outer space. I imagined swirling galaxies of nebulas and planets and dark matter. Each time, I was filled with this overwhelming feeling of being a tiny insignificant speck in the cosmos. Perhaps that’s why, when I first read The Library of Babel, I was so perplexed by this new idea of infinity. <br/><br/>Jorge Luis Borges' The Library of Babel describes the universe as an infinite lib</span>
  <span id="freeText16786817710461195518" style="display:none"> The concept of infinity never fails to perplex me. Previously, my visions of infinity were focused mainly on the idea of outer space. I imagined swirling galaxies of nebulas and planets and dark matter. Each time, I was filled with this overwhelming feeling of being a tiny insignificant speck in the cosmos. Perhaps that’s why, when I first read The Library of Babel, I was so perplexed by this new idea of infinity. <br /><br />Jorge Luis Borges' The Library of Babel describes the universe as an infinite library. The library is composed of an indefinite number of identical hexagonal galleries. The wall of each hexagon contains five bookshelves. Each bookshelf contains thirty-two books, each with 410 pages. The only 25 symbols found is each book are the comma, period, space, and twenty two letters of the alphabet. For the most part, the books in the Library of Babel are filled with gibberish with the twenty five symbols arranged in random orders. However, with the library being infinite in size, it contains the complete expression of all words and every story. The narrator was on a quest in search of a book as a young man, but he has grown old in the library and is preparing to die. <br /><br />The Library of Babel gave me a new contrasting sense of perhaps how to view infinity. In the cosmos, I imagine an infinite expanse of dark nothingness, but in the Library of Babel, there would be an infinite collection of knowledge and information surrounding me. Reading The Library of Babel, I envisioned the library to be akin to an impossible maze - each turn into a new gallery would present an identical view of bookshelves. Being stuck in an infinite library initially seemed like a dream to me, but as I read on, I realized that the identical hexagonal rooms with identical bookshelves and identical book-forms would push me to the brink of lunacy. If I were stuck in the Library of Babel, I would likely wander off and become lost, and eventually feel an overwhelming sense of personal insignificance in comparison to the uncountable books and information around me.<br /><br />I think the idea I’m trying to encapsulate in this stream of consciousness-style reflection is that reading Jorge Luis Borges’ work created a new perspective to think about infinity. Perhaps I’ll reread The Library of Babel in a few year and glean more from what Jorge Luis Borges was trying to convey about infinity. I’d previously never read any of Jorge Luis Borges’, but The Library of Babel was definitely a fascinating first read. <br /><br />Link to my review + other reviews on my blog:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://thestorystash.wixsite.com/thestorystash/thestorystashhome/my-thoughts-on-the-library-of-babel">https://thestorystash.wixsite.com/the...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16786817710461195518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_467780237" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/467780237" />
    <a title="Jordan Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6089955-jordan-taylor"><img alt="Jordan Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573051219p2/6089955.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/467780237?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jordan Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jordan Taylor" href="/user/show/6089955-jordan-taylor">Jordan Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jordan by: </span>
      Ryan Carter
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=books-owned">books-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=ocean-deep">ocean-deep</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=silmarillion-league">silmarillion-league</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=the-search-for-beauty">the-search-for-beauty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=writer-from-argentina">writer-from-argentina</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=published-1940-s">published-1940-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6089955-jordan-taylor?shelf=vivid-books">vivid-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer467780237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13341905061114986718">It has been a year or two since I have last read anything by my favorite author of all time, Jorge Luis Borges.<br/>"The Library of Babel" was always one of the short stories that stayed with me, and I am glad that I decided to re-read it last night.<br/>Here, the Universe and the Library are one and the same, and the world is made up of shelves upon shelves of countless, infinite numbers of books.<br/>The books, however, do not contain stories and histories and vast knowledge. Or, perhaps they do. In fact, ma</span>
  <span id="freeText13341905061114986718" style="display:none">It has been a year or two since I have last read anything by my favorite author of all time, Jorge Luis Borges.<br />"The Library of Babel" was always one of the short stories that stayed with me, and I am glad that I decided to re-read it last night.<br />Here, the Universe and the Library are one and the same, and the world is made up of shelves upon shelves of countless, infinite numbers of books.<br />The books, however, do not contain stories and histories and vast knowledge. Or, perhaps they do. In fact, many scholars insist that they must. Sadly, however, no meaning has yet been found in their pages. The books are a jumbled, random, confusing mess of letters and commas and periods that make no sense.<br />Here, books are things to be studied, to have theories about, and to experiment upon. They are not so much literary as they are scientific. The people of Babel known that the books are important, that the books define them. They simply cannot figure out how.<br />There appear to be so many varying views about the books, they are a highly controversial topic. Irrelevant or vital? There are those who worship books but cannot read, those who believe in a secret Librarian somewhere who has unlocked the Library's secrets, those who go on pilgrimages to try and find "their" book, Inquisitors who destroy books with offensive letters, and still more.<br />Borges does a marvelous job here of writing an allegorical story in which books parallel religion and the controversial debate of creation.<br />No conclusion is reached in the story, but rather you come away from it with pages of questions that you may not have thought of before.<br />I loved that Borges slipped in a mirror, right on the first page - of course one had to make an appearance somewhere!<br />Two quotes jumped out at me this time...<br />"When I am dead, compassionate hands will throw me over the railing; my tomb will be the unfathomable air, my body will sink for ages, and will decay and dissolve in the wind engendered by my fall, which shall be infinite."<br />And..<br />"There was no problem whose eloquent solution did not exist - somewhere in some hexagon... The universe suddenly became congruent with the unlimited width and breadth of humankind's hope."<br />I absolutely love Borges, above any and all other writer of all time. I think that his are the only books that I will always, always read, over and over, for the rest of my life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13341905061114986718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Xjt3EOcOO+RQAAgJqenfoXwX4RsV4rcEs9BV6AAN2cY9eJZDfm3d46gro5qvbHxdSPT3tSPrs944ohGC1hkMBA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lTAT+rhDt0djIOLoNwCEg28096sqGViheE15fPHHhLL2c/KpISBRQJsLSXsxhSd/W9fhBRwQXHvzPz0WJ9NRcA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

O\'Connor herself singled it out by making it the title piece of her first collection and the story she most often chose for readings or talks to students. It is a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8846915331374621550\" style=\"display:none\">A Good Man Is Hard to Find is Flannery O\'Connor\'s most famous and most discussed story. O\'Connor herself singled it out by making it the title piece of her first collection and the story she most often chose for readings or talks to students. It is an unforgettable tale, both riveting and comic, of the confrontation of a family with violence and sudden death. More than anything else O\'Connor ever wrote, this story mixes the comedy, violence, and religious concerns that characterize her fiction.\n\nThis casebook for the story includes an introduction by the editor, a chronology of the author\'s life, the authoritative text of the story itself, comments and letters by O\'Connor about the story, critical essays, and a bibliography. The critical essays span more than twenty years of commentary and suggest several approaches to the story--formalistic, thematic, deconstructionist-- all within the grasp of the undergraduate, while the introduction also points interested students toward still other resources. Useful for both beginning and advanced students, this casebook provides an in-depth introduction to one of America\'s most gifted modern writers.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8846915331374621550\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges">About Jorge Luis Borges</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1537559279p3/500.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12908772505725729158">Jorge Francisco Isidoro Luis Borges Acevedo, usually referred to as Jorge Luis Borges (Spanish pronunciation: [xoɾxe lwis boɾxes]), was an Argentine writer and poet born in Buenos Aires. In 1914, his family moved to Switzerland where he attended school and traveled to Spain. On his return to Argentina in 1921, Borges began publishing his poems and essays in Surrealist literary journals. He also wo</span>
  <span id="freeText12908772505725729158" style="display:none">Jorge Francisco Isidoro Luis Borges Acevedo, usually referred to as Jorge Luis Borges (Spanish pronunciation: [xoɾxe lwis boɾxes]), was an Argentine writer and poet born in Buenos Aires. In 1914, his family moved to Switzerland where he attended school and traveled to Spain. On his return to Argentina in 1921, Borges began publishing his poems and essays in Surrealist literary journals. He also worked as a librarian and public lecturer. Borges was fluent in several languages. He was a target of political persecution during the Peron regime, and supported the military juntas that overthrew it.<br /><br />Due to a hereditary condition, Borges became blind in his late fifties. In 1955, he was appointed director of the National Public Library (Biblioteca Nacional) and professor of Literature at the University of Buenos Aires. In 1961, he came to international attention when he received the first International Publishers' Prize Prix Formentor. His work was translated and published widely in the United States and in Europe. He died in Geneva, Switzerland, in 1986.<br /><br />J. M. Coetzee said of Borges: "He, more than anyone, renovated the language of fiction and thus opened the way to a remarkable generation of Spanish American novelists." </span>
  <a data-text-id="12908772505725729158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
